Chris the trick is to praise the food but mention something else as being your favorite or mention that something else they made was even better.  This  subtle points the chef towards what you really like, while not insulting what you tried.  Its indirect criticism of what you had as not being quite as good as something else, even though you think it was "good".  You will see Japanese people do this sometimes where they praise the food but then go on to talk about foods they really love as a flow of conversation making mention of their absolute favorites.  Smart chefs like one sushi chef who was preparing sushi for me, would present me with two dishes at once and ask which i preferred, then narrowing down what I really like and what my preferred taste was.  It was a great experience by the Kamogawa river, in Kyoto.  Taking a lesson from this, you might want to order two small dishes and praise them both but praise the one you truly do like far more than the one you like less.

I'm 36 now and I weigh 76kg, being 1,78 meters tall. In summer 2020 after three months of being chained to my lockdown home office desk, I came in on roughly...84kg. I usually run 40 km per week. In summer its sometimes more in winter its less (and I mostly run on the treamill in winter). During the lockdowns I couldnt go to the gym and jogging/running outsome was forbidden for some time. That made me realize how unhealthy my diet is. I mean if one gains 8 kg in three months or so, just because he doesnt go running in that time, that surely isnt normal. So my advise is that you cut down on calories. Losing weight just from doing sports hardly ever works out for the most poeple. I dont eat anything fried anymore (including chips) and I only eat bread 2 or 3 times a week, instead of 7 times. This simple change gave me such much more energy and it helps with the eye circles, which come from to many carbs.

Then Covid happened and many people didn't want to share the responsibility. I'm from the UK and we were (and still are) probably the worst country dealing with the pandemic, from top to bottom. Every summer, we wanted to plan a trip again but due to that selfish behaviour, people kept spreading the virus even harder. Some people we know of said that the whole thing is a lie and they refused to be vaccinated. It makes it quite hard to respect that decision since it affects you and everyone else in a long run.   Fast forward to the present time, we are nagging about how strict is Japan, why they don't want tourists over and so on. Even if it makes us very sad to not be able to visit Japan soon, I completely understand and agree with that decision. And I'm not sure why would anyone want to visit a place where they are not very welcome. My big concern is that this isolationist policy will basically turn into a new Sakoku Edict (or Sakoku Decree, for us Genshin Impact fans), in all but name.  Frankly, as you said, it's frustrating that Japanese citizens get to travel the world, but won't allow foreigners into the country.  It is also equally frustrating that they export so much of their culture and cuisine, but are reluctant or outright refusing to let outside influences enter their own country.  But if Japan really wants to stand on the world stage as a prominent member of geopolitics, they can't keep catering to their elderly population for much longer.  More and more of their population is going to start dying off as they get older.  They need to start bringing younger generations to the negotiating table, and letting them take part in the political discussion.  And I'm talking about people in their mid to late-20s, roughly around the time people are eligible to run for office here in America.  I think a great example of the consequences of this ineffective approach of catering too much to the older generations can be seen in the 2016 movie Shin Godzilla (which, I recently learned, Sharla worked on as an English translator).  A bloated, aging government stuck in the past was unable to deal with an unprecedented crisis, and it resulted in the government basically being destroyed by Godzilla's atomic breath.  It took the younger generation of government officials with fresher ideas and didn't give much consideration to things like age and seniority (like so many of the older generations do) to save the day.  Basically, the people who do stand out, didn't follow the crowd, and spoke their mind freely.  To me, the message was that if Japan wants to survive and thrive, it needs to cater more to its' younger generations who may not necessarily share the outdated views and values of the older generations.  Ergo, they need to listen to the concerns that younger Japanese citizens have and properly address them, such as the rising costs of living, healthcare, childcare, being able to work in non-exploitative companies, and more equality for minorities (especially women, who are still basically treated as second-class citizens in the country, despite many of them being smarter than their male counterparts).  So far, doubling down on the failed policies of the past that got them to where they are now (an economic slump that has lasted for decades) will not get them out of the mess they're in.  Simply put, they need newer, fresher ideas from their younger generations in order to pull themselves out of their slump and truly became a world leader again.

Absolutely not true about having to say every dish is delicious, especially if you're a foreigner in Japan.  Nobody expects you to find every dish amazing.  There's just as much enjoyment and fun in people finding things disgusting, like in any other culture.  Even amongst Japanese people there are regional differences, look at natto popularity in Kansai or Kanto.  There is no expectation for visitor or native alike to blindly spout how much you love something when you find it unpalatable, and politely declining something is fully within normal social bounds.  Of course, if you do want a universal reaction of disgust, offer a Japanese person licorice - spitting it out and saying "mazui" is a common reaction!   As for Japanese TV shows _about food_ talking about food... well, yeah, they're food shows.  Try watching other TV shows.  "How Do You Like Wednesday?" is a really nice show for getting into Japanese humor, seeing a good slice of Japanese culture over the past 20 years, and the country itself as it involves a lot of travel - it's basically a YouTube channel before YouTube existed.

Hey Chris you should try tracking everything you eat for at least 3 months - including calories. Enter your details on a TDEE calculator to find out what your maintenance calories are and aim to eat ~500 less each day. This is a lot more important than heavy exercise, in fact walking is the best exercise to lose weight as it does not make you overly hungry and will keep your energy levels stable. Personally I recommend eating a low carb breakfast (I always have a 4 egg omelette which sets me up for 28g protein and around 280 calories) and then snack on 2-3 peices of fruit in between meals if you have to. If you can't get access to a low carb breakfast I would actually recommend fasting until lunch, then supplementing some extra protein. Also don't go too low in healthy fats as they are good for satiety. Ideally you want to eat most of your carbs later in the day (this is called carb back-loading and has been shown to be effective at improving body composition, burning body fat during the day and providing your muscles with enough carbs to build muscle while resting).

Health and fitness is not achieved by a strenuous crash diet/training period. It is achieved by small steps on a daily basis: one less cake, quit smoking, do 10 more minutes of exercises and all that. In time you could add volume to your training, or work harder on the diet to get to some fat percentage, for example, but those are more advanced goals that should be pursued later. If you crash-do something you are going to rebound right after the strenuous period. If you did "restore" your health by working with a plan over the next two years, rather than four months, you'd be delivering an incredible message. Think about that.

The other problem with visiting as a tourist at the moment is you need a visa. Here in the UK you either have to do this in person or use an expensive visa agency. If you do do it in person you have to travel to the embassy (or one of a few consulates) both to queue and drop off the form and then again a week or so later to pick it up. On top of that the UK embassy has been overwhelmed with demand, mainly I think from students like our son who have had their study abroad postponed several times, such that appointments were going within seconds. So tourism from the UK only going to become feasible in larger numbers once the visa waiver is back in place.  There was also the need to get (and worry about failing) the preflight PCR test but at least that rule is being phased out, luckily the day before he leaves.

Just remember, Chris. Weight loss is only about 10% from physical exercise. The only real way to lose weight is to eat healthy. i.e. No vegetable oils, no sugar, no breads. They don't BAN tourists.  You can still visit Japan, however, it is with a tour guide/group.  You pay for a trip package and they take you to places they have prebooked and scheduled a head of time.  So no, you can't go by yourself and go wherever you want in the country, like shopping in shibuya, but you do get to still see Japan. The difference is you are with a tour guide and on a tour bus for the entire trip and are with other people from different countries. You all experience the same trip.

I think Japan not opening goes beyond politics, I think it's a predictable cultural problem, particularly since the Japanese aren't very vulnerable to covid since almost all of them lack a gene that makes it worse.    I think the reason Japan wont open is a combination of; group identity over individuality; fear of the new/risk aversion; and fear of being seen as wrong. Those huge parts of Japanese culture as far as I have seen. Add them all together and it makes sense to have a large part of the population that doesn't like to be seen as being part of a group that is in the wrong, or has people in it that are in the wrong, and don't want to be blamed for a (relatively) "new scary thing" like covid. So when "scary new thing" acts up they try to blame some other group or, just flat out try to deny any problem with their own group. So, no more foreigners because they want to stop covid spreading, but also want to blame foreigners for spreading covid, and don't want to accept that one, covid is something that can be lived with because its still "new" and therefor scary, and two, most spread is going to be down to the majority, the Japanese themselves.  Other examples where I think this cultural problem shows are; the ridiculous police conviction rate, because its culturally unacceptable for the police to be seen as wrong; the societies refusal to take sexual assault seriously because "we Japanese men can't be that bad, it can't be that serious! *buries head in sand*"; timidness in classrooms where kids, older kids in particular, are afraid to speak up, and any that do and give a wrong answer tend to be cruelly mocked and bullied by the rest of the class; the tendency for communities to investigate foreigners for wrong doing before they investigate Japanese, even when a Japanese person is in the wrong, or even consistently in the wrong; the extreme reaction following WW2 when Japan was absolutely, undeniably in the wrong, following which they went so contrite as to go pacifist (which is only now barely wearing off), and then also tried to forget about the shameful parts and blank out much of it.

I've been watching through Doctor Who, and it strikes me that the Brits ALL seem to really really REALLY love Christmas. More than Americans do certainly. And as a Jewish American, I feel weird with really enthusiastic Christmas celebration because in the US that tends to come with a heavy dose of Christianity. I remember when My dad watched Harry Potter for the first time he was offended by the fact that the whole Hogwarts castle celebrated the birth of the baby Jesus. He reasoned that the witches and wizards should have HATED all of Christianity for all the persecutions and burnings of witches and claims that their magic was from being in league with the devil; that Hogwarts celebrating Christmas would be like Jews celebrating the birth of Hitler.   But the more I engage with British media, the more I realize it's that Christmas to the British is like... not Christian at all.  It seems to be an AGGRESSIVELY secular holiday. Like, the average secular Brit doesn't treat it as any more religious than Americans do with Halloween. essentially  But it also makes sense because Christmas is actually just secular saturnalia re-claimed by the Church. I do sort of wish that we could all stop calling it Christmas and start calling it Saturnalia because Jesus was born in the summer and Christmas is tacitly NOT a Christian holiday, so why is "Christ" in it?
